<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><b>Website</b><br/>

<p><b>Donald Ervin Knuth</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/uː/ long 'oo' in 'food'">uː</span><span title="/θ/ 'th' in 'thigh'">θ</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faq_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-faq-4">[4]</a></sup> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i>kə-<b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">NOOTH</span></span></b></i></a></span>; born January 10, 1938) is an American <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer scientist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Professor_emeritus" title="Professor emeritus">professor emeritus</a> at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>.</p>
<p>He is the author of the multi-volume work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Art_of_Computer_Programming" title="The Art of Computer Programming">The Art of Computer Programming</a></i>. He contributed to the development of the rigorous analysis of the computational complexity of algorithms and systematized formal mathematical techniques for it. In the process he also popularized the <a href="/wiki/Big_O_notation" title="Big O notation">asymptotic notation</a>. In addition to fundamental contributions in several branches of <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">theoretical computer science</a>, Knuth is the creator of the <a href="/wiki/TeX" title="TeX">TeX</a> computer typesetting system, the related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/METAFONT" title="METAFONT">METAFONT</a> font definition language and rendering system, and the <a href="/wiki/Computer_Modern" title="Computer Modern">Computer Modern</a> family of typefaces.</p>
<p>As a writer and scholar, Knuth created the <a href="/wiki/WEB" title="WEB">WEB</a> and <a href="/wiki/CWEB" title="CWEB">CWEB</a> computer programming systems designed to encourage and facilitate <a href="/wiki/Literate_programming" title="Literate programming">literate programming</a>, and designed the <a href="/wiki/MIX" title="MIX">MIX</a>/<a href="/wiki/MMIX" title="MMIX">MMIX</a> <a href="/wiki/Instruction_set" title="Instruction set">instruction set architectures</a>. Knuth strongly opposes granting <a href="/wiki/Software_patent" title="Software patent">software patents</a>, having expressed his opinion to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="United States Patent and Trademark Office">United States Patent and Trademark Office</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Patent_Organization" title="European Patent Organization">European Patent Organization</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Knuth was born in <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee" title="Milwaukee">Milwaukee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, to German-Americans Ervin Henry Knuth and Louise Marie Bohning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> His father had two jobs: running a small printing company and teaching bookkeeping at <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Lutheran_High_School" title="Milwaukee Lutheran High School">Milwaukee Lutheran High School</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raskin2013_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raskin2013-6">[6]</a></sup> Donald, a student at Milwaukee Lutheran High School, received academic accolades there, especially because of the ingenious ways that he thought of solving problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raskin2013_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raskin2013-6">[6]</a></sup> For example, in eighth grade, he entered a contest to find the number of words that the letters in "Ziegler's Giant Bar" could be rearranged to create. Although the judges only had 2,500 words on their list, Donald found 4,500 words, winning the contest. As prizes, the school received a new television and enough candy bars for all of his schoolmates to eat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raskin2013_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Raskin2013-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Knuth had a difficult time choosing physics over music as his major at <a href="/wiki/Case_Western_Reserve_University" title="Case Western Reserve University">Case Institute of Technology</a> (now part of <a href="/wiki/Case_Western_Reserve_University" title="Case Western Reserve University">Case Western Reserve University</a>). He also joined Beta Nu Chapter of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theta_Chi_fraternity" title="Theta Chi fraternity">Theta Chi fraternity</a>. While studying physics at the Case Institute of Technology, Knuth was introduced to the <a href="/wiki/IBM_650" title="IBM 650">IBM 650</a>, one of the early <a href="/wiki/Mainframe_computer" title="Mainframe computer">mainframes</a>. After reading the computer's manual, Knuth decided to rewrite the assembly and compiler code for the machine used in his school, because he believed he could do it better.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koshy2004_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koshy2004-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1958, Knuth created a program to help his school's basketball team win their games. He assigned "values" to players in order to gauge their probability of getting points, a novel approach that <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/CBS_Evening_News" title="CBS Evening News">CBS Evening News</a></i> later reported on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koshy2004_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koshy2004-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Knuth was one of the founding editors of the <i>Engineering and Science Review</i>, which won a national award as best technical magazine in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beta_Nu_Chapter_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beta_Nu_Chapter-9">[9]</a></sup> He then switched from physics to mathematics, and in 1960 he received his bachelor of science degree, simultaneously being given a master of science degree by a special award of the faculty who considered his work exceptionally outstanding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koshy2004_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Koshy2004-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turing_Award_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turing_Award-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1963, with mathematician <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Hall_(mathematician)" title="Marshall Hall (mathematician)">Marshall Hall</a> as his adviser,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mathgene_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-mathgene-1">[1]</a></sup> he earned a PhD in mathematics from the <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">California Institute of Technology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_work">Early work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After receiving his PhD, Knuth joined Caltech's faculty as an associate professor.</p>
<p>He accepted a commission to write a book on computer <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming language</a> <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compilers</a>. While working on this project, Knuth decided that he could not adequately treat the topic without first developing a fundamental theory of computer programming, which became <i><a href="/wiki/The_Art_of_Computer_Programming" title="The Art of Computer Programming">The Art of Computer Programming</a></i>. He originally planned to publish this as a single book. As Knuth developed his outline for the book, he concluded that he required six volumes, and then seven, to thoroughly cover the subject. He published the first volume in 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Just before publishing the first volume of <i>The Art of Computer Programming</i>, Knuth left Caltech to accept employment with the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Defense_Analyses#Center_for_Communications_and_Computing" title="Institute for Defense Analyses">Institute for Defense Analyses' Communications Research Division</a>, then situated on the <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> campus, which was performing mathematical research in <a href="/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">cryptography</a> to support the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a>.</p>
<p>Knuth then left this position to join the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> faculty, where he is now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Professor_emeritus" title="Professor emeritus">professor emeritus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homepage_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-homepage-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Writings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Knuth was a writer as well as a computer scientist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Knuth has been called the "father of the <a href="/wiki/Analysis_of_algorithms" title="Analysis of algorithms">analysis of algorithms</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Art_of_Computer_Programming_.28TAOCP.29"><i>The Art of Computer Programming</i> (<i>TAOCP</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Art of Computer Programming (TAOCP)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Art_of_Computer_Programming" title="The Art of Computer Programming">The Art of Computer Programming</a></div>
<p>In the 1970s, Knuth described computer science as "a totally new field with no real identity. And the standard of available publications was not that high. A lot of the papers coming out were quite simply wrong. [...] So one of my motivations was to put straight a story that had been very badly told."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> After producing the third volume of his series in 1976, he expressed such frustration with the nascent state of the then newly developed electronic publishing tools (especially those that provided input to phototypesetters) that he took time out to work on typesetting and created the <a href="/wiki/TeX" title="TeX">TeX</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/METAFONT" title="METAFONT">METAFONT</a> tools. By 2013, the first three volumes and part one of volume four of his series had been published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Concrete_Mathematics" title="Concrete Mathematics">Concrete Mathematics: A Foundation for Computer Science</a></i> 2nd ed., which originated with an expansion of the mathematical preliminaries section of Volume 1 of <i>TAoCP</i>, has also been published.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bill_Gates" title="Bill Gates">Bill Gates</a> has praised the difficulty of the subject matter in <i>The Art of Computer Programming</i>, stating that "If you think you're a really good programmer...You should definitely send me a résumé if you can read the whole thing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_works">Other works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Other works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>He is also the author of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surreal_Numbers_(book)" title="Surreal Numbers (book)">Surreal Numbers</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-surreal_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-surreal-19">[19]</a></sup> a mathematical novelette on <a href="/wiki/John_Horton_Conway" title="John Horton Conway">John Conway</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a> construction of an alternate system of numbers. Instead of simply explaining the subject, the book seeks to show the development of the mathematics. Knuth wanted the book to prepare students for doing original, creative research.</p>
<p>In 1995, Knuth wrote the foreword to the book <i>A=B</i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marko_Petkovsek" title="Marko Petkovsek">Marko Petkovsek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Wilf" title="Herbert Wilf">Herbert Wilf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Doron_Zeilberger" title="Doron Zeilberger">Doron Zeilberger</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Knuth is also an occasional contributor of language puzzles to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Word_Ways:_The_Journal_of_Recreational_Linguistics" title="Word Ways: The Journal of Recreational Linguistics">Word Ways: The Journal of Recreational Linguistics</a></i>.</p>
<p>Knuth has also delved into <a href="/wiki/Recreational_mathematics" title="Recreational mathematics">recreational mathematics</a>. He contributed articles to the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Recreational_Mathematics" title="Journal of Recreational Mathematics">Journal of Recreational Mathematics</a></i> beginning in the 1960s, and was acknowledged as a major contributor in <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Madachy" title="Joseph Madachy">Joseph Madachy</a>'s <i>Mathematics on Vacation</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Works_regarding_Knuth.27s_religious_beliefs">Works regarding Knuth's religious beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Works regarding Knuth's religious beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to his writings on computer science, Knuth, a <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlatoni2006_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlatoni2006-22">[22]</a></sup> is also the author of <i>3:16 Bible Texts Illuminated</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> in which he examines the Bible by a process of <a href="/wiki/Systematic_sampling" title="Systematic sampling">systematic sampling</a>, namely an analysis of chapter 3, verse 16 of each book. Each verse is accompanied by a rendering in calligraphic art, contributed by a group of calligraphers under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Zapf" title="Hermann Zapf">Hermann Zapf</a>. Subsequently he was invited to give a set of lectures on his 3:16 project, resulting in another book, <i><a href="/wiki/Things_a_Computer_Scientist_Rarely_Talks_About" title="Things a Computer Scientist Rarely Talks About">Things a Computer Scientist Rarely Talks About</a></i>, where he published the lectures <i>"God and Computer Science"</i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opinion_on_software_patents">Opinion on software patents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Opinion on software patents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a member of the academic and scientific community, Knuth is strongly opposed to the policy of granting <a href="/wiki/Software_patent" title="Software patent">software patents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> He has expressed his disagreement directly to both the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="United States Patent and Trademark Office">United States Patent and Trademark Office</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Patent_Organization" title="European Patent Organization">European Patent Organization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_Musings">Computer Musings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Computer Musings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Knuth gives informal lectures a few times a year at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, which he called Computer Musings. He is a visiting professor at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Department_of_Computer_Science" title="Oxford University Department of Computer Science">Oxford University Department of Computer Science</a> in the United Kingdom and an Honorary Fellow of <a href="/wiki/Magdalen_College,_Oxford" title="Magdalen College, Oxford">Magdalen College</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_name">Chinese name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Chinese name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Knuth's <a href="/wiki/Chinese_name" title="Chinese name">Chinese name</a> is Gao Dena (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">高德纳</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">高德納</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Gāo dé nà</span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faq_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-faq-4">[4]</a></sup> In 1977, he was given this name by <a href="/wiki/Frances_Yao" title="Frances Yao">Frances Yao</a>, shortly before making a 3-week trip to <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faq_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-faq-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Art_of_Computer_Programming_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Art_of_Computer_Programming-29">[29]</a></sup> In his 1980 volume of <i>The Art of Computer Programming</i> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">计算机程序设计艺术</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">電腦程式設計藝術</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Jìsuànjī chéngxù shèjì yìshù</span></i>), Knuth explains that he embraced his Chinese name because he wanted to be known by the growing numbers of computer programmers in China at the time. In 1989, his Chinese name was placed atop the <i>Journal of Computer Science and Technology</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> header, which Knuth says "makes me feel close to all Chinese people although I cannot speak your language."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Art_of_Computer_Programming_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Art_of_Computer_Programming-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_concerns">Health concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Health concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2006, Knuth was diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a>. He underwent surgery in December that year and started "a little bit of radiation therapy... as a precaution but the prognosis looks pretty good", as he reported in his video autobiography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancer_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-cancer-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Humor">Humor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Humor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Knuth-check2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="900" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Knuth-check2.png/220px-Knuth-check2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Knuth-check2.png/330px-Knuth-check2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Knuth-check2.png/440px-Knuth-check2.png 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<p>Knuth used to pay a <a href="/wiki/Finder%27s_fee" title="Finder's fee">finder's fee</a> of $2.56 for any typographical errors or mistakes discovered in his books, because "256 pennies is one <a href="/wiki/Hexadecimal" title="Hexadecimal">hexadecimal</a> dollar", and $0.32 for "valuable suggestions". According to an article in the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a>'s <i>Technology Review</i>, these <a href="/wiki/Knuth_reward_check" title="Knuth reward check">Knuth reward checks</a> are "among computerdom's most prized trophies". Knuth had to stop sending real checks in 2008 due to bank fraud, and instead now gives each error finder a "certificate of deposit" from a publicly listed balance in his fictitious "Bank of <a href="/wiki/San_Serriffe" title="San Serriffe">San Serriffe</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>He once warned a correspondent, "Beware of bugs in the above code; I have only proved it correct, not tried it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Knuth published his first "scientific" article in a school magazine in 1957 under the title "<a href="/wiki/Potrzebie" title="Potrzebie">Potrzebie</a> System of Weights and Measures." In it, he defined the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_unit" title="Fundamental unit">fundamental unit</a> of <a href="/wiki/Length" title="Length">length</a> as the thickness of <i><a href="/wiki/Mad_(magazine)" title="Mad (magazine)">Mad</a></i> No. 26, and named the fundamental unit of <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> "whatmeworry." <i>Mad</i> published the article in issue No. 33 (June 1957).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>To demonstrate the concept of <a href="/wiki/Recursion" title="Recursion">recursion</a>, Knuth intentionally referred "Circular definition" and "Definition, circular" to each other in the index of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Art_of_Computer_Programming" title="The Art of Computer Programming">The Art of Computer Programming</a>, Volume 1</i>.</p>
<p>At the TUG 2010 Conference, Knuth announced an <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">XML</a>-based successor to TeX, titled "iTeX" (<small>pronounced </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[iː˨˩˦tɛks˧˥]</a></span>, with a bell ringing), which would support features such as arbitrarily scaled irrational units, <a href="/wiki/3D_printing" title="3D printing">3D printing</a>, animation, and stereophonic sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Knuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Awards and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1971, Knuth was the recipient of the first <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">ACM</a> <a href="/wiki/Grace_Murray_Hopper_Award" title="Grace Murray Hopper Award">Grace Murray Hopper Award</a>. He has received various other awards including the <a href="/wiki/Turing_Award" title="Turing Award">Turing Award</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann_Medal" title="John von Neumann Medal">John von Neumann Medal</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Prize" title="Kyoto Prize">Kyoto Prize</a>.</p>
<p>In recognition of Knuth's contributions to the field of computer science, in 1990 he was awarded the one-of-a-kind academic title of <i>Professor of The Art of Computer Programming</i>, which has since been revised to <i>Professor <a href="/wiki/Emeritus" title="Emeritus">Emeritus</a> of The Art of Computer Programming</i>.</p>
<p>Knuth was elected to the <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> in 1975. In 1992, he became an associate of the <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">French Academy of Sciences</a>. Also that year, he retired from regular research and teaching at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> in order to finish <i><a href="/wiki/The_Art_of_Computer_Programming" title="The Art of Computer Programming">The Art of Computer Programming</a></i>. He was elected a <a href="/wiki/List_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_elected_in_2003" title="List of Fellows of the Royal Society elected in 2003">Foreign Member of the Royal Society (ForMemRS) in 2003</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-formemrs_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-formemrs-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Knuth was elected as a Fellow (first class of Fellows) of the <a href="/wiki/Society_for_Industrial_and_Applied_Mathematics" title="Society for Industrial and Applied Mathematics">Society for Industrial and Applied Mathematics</a> in 2009 for his outstanding contributions to mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> He is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Academy_of_Science_and_Letters" title="Norwegian Academy of Science and Letters">Norwegian Academy of Science and Letters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In 2012, he became a fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Mathematical_Society" title="American Mathematical Society">American Mathematical Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Other awards and honors include:</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 35em; -webkit-column-width: 35em; column-width: 35em;">
<ul>
<li>First <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">ACM</a> <a href="/wiki/Grace_Murray_Hopper_Award" title="Grace Murray Hopper Award">Grace Murray Hopper Award</a>, 1971</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Turing_Award" title="Turing Award">Turing Award</a>, 1974</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lester_R._Ford_Award" title="Lester R. Ford Award">Lester R. Ford Award</a>, 1975<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and 1993<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li>Josiah Willard Gibbs Lecturer, 1978<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a>, 1979</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Franklin_Medal" title="Franklin Medal">Franklin Medal</a>, 1988</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann_Medal" title="John von Neumann Medal">John von Neumann Medal</a>, 1995</li>
<li>Harvey Prize from the <a href="/wiki/Technion_%E2%80%93_Israel_Institute_of_Technology" title="Technion – Israel Institute of Technology">Technion</a>, 1995<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Prize" title="Kyoto Prize">Kyoto Prize</a>, 1996</li>
<li>Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/Computer_History_Museum" title="Computer History Museum">Computer History Museum</a> "for his fundamental early work in the history of computing algorithms, development of the TeX typesetting language, and for major contributions to mathematics and computer science." 1998<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ComputerHistory_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ComputerHistory-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li>Katayanagi Prize, 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/BBVA_Foundation_Frontiers_of_Knowledge_Award" title="BBVA Foundation Frontiers of Knowledge Award">BBVA Foundation Frontiers of Knowledge Award</a> in the category of Information and Communication Technologies, 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Turing_Lecture" title="Turing Lecture">Turing Lecture</a>, 2011</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Stanford_University_School_of_Engineering" title="Stanford University School of Engineering">Stanford University School of Engineering</a> Hero Award, 2011<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-The_Art_of_Computer_Programming-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-The_Art_of_Computer_Programming_29-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-The_Art_of_Computer_Programming_29-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Knuth, Donald Ervin (1980). <i>计算机程序设计技巧 (Ji suan ji cheng xu she ji ji qiao)</i> [<i>The Art of Computer Programming</i>]. Translated by Guan, JiWen; Su, Yunlin. Beijing: Defense Industry Publishing Co. <q>I fondly hope that many Chinese computer programmers will learn to recognize my Chinese name Gao Dena, which was given to me by Francis Yao just before I visited your country in 1977. I still have very fond memories of that three-week visit, and I have been glad to see Gao Dena on the masthead of the <i>Journal of Computer Science and Technology</i> since 1989. This name makes me feel close to all Chinese people although I cannot speak your language.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADonald+Knuth&amp;rft.aufirst=Donald+Ervin&amp;rft.aulast=Knuth&amp;rft.btitle=%E8%AE%A1%E7%AE%97%E6%9C%BA%E7%A8%E5%BA%8F%E8%AE%BE%E8%AE%A1%E6%8A%E5%B7%A7+%28Ji+suan+ji+cheng+xu+she+ji+ji+qiao%29&amp;rft.date=1980&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.place=Beijing&amp;rft.pub=Defense+Industry+Publishing+Co.&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
